B0053Q9DHW|5.0|I am a virgin to Jane Eyre, having never read the Charlotte Bront novel nor seen any of the multitudes of cinematic adaptations that were bore from it. Counting only the adaptations that share the same name, this 2011 version is the tenth film based on the novel, although several of the early ones are lost to time. This latest vision boasts a California-born director and an Australian and a German in the lead roles. Not the most likely people you'd expect for the adaptation of a thoroughly English novel, but once the film begins it quickly becomes irrelevant. It's with sadness that I realize how long I was unaware of the greatness of Jane Eyre, but with glee that it took this film to open my eyes.     For many, the plot of Jane Eyre may be a product of common sense rather than acquired knowledge, but I confess that I knew very little before endeavoring to watch it. For those like me, I'll attempt to keep elements of the plot brief so as to not spoil anything. The film opens with the present Jane Eyre (played by Mia Wasikowska) and allows the core of the story to unfold in flashback. Jane grows up an orphan, raised by her cruel aunt (Sally Hawkins) and is eventually sent to Lowood, a charity school for girls, with a rigidly sadistic faculty. Once she leaves Lowood Jane is invited by the sprawling, isolated manor Thornfield Hall. Thornfield Hall's housekeeper Mrs. Fairfax (Judi Dench) offers Jane a job as a governess over the master's ward, a young French girl named Adele. The mysterious master is Edward Rochester (Michael Fassbender), who, after a short time, reveals himself at the manor. Despite his position as master and Jane's position as subordinate, the two strike up a rapport as equals despite there being something markedly sinister about Mr. Rochester.     Jane Eyre is directed by Cary Joji Fukunaga, whose film debut, the Spanish-language Sin Nombre, was released to great acclaim in 2009. A Californian director of Japanese and Swedish descent may not be theobvious choice for this adaptation, but his direction is flawless. From the fade in, Fukunaga instantly captures the tone with a perfect dreary, gloomy look that creates the most effective kind of cinematic atmosphere. To simply label Jane Eyre atmospheric is to understate its effectiveness. It's enveloping and evocative, gaining your complete attention with its affirmed stranglehold on the essence of Gothic Romance.     A period drama typically emphasizes tone and character over simple narrative and with performances that are nothing short of hypnotic, this is no exception. Wasikowska is flawless as the title character, appropriately vulnerable but with a commanding sense of confidence. After rising to prominence in the billion-dollar hit Alice in Wonderland, she has steadily made her mark as an actress in much smaller films. She has the fame, but she seems determined to get the respect and thus far she's succeeding admirably. Michael Fassbender has also appeared in small, yet consistently great, films while flirting cautiously with the mainstream. Here, as in other roles, he has almost radioactive presence. When Rochester appears, he's not a suave, charming fellow but a blunt, formal, and stern man. Simultaneously, he does possess an intense, almost frightening, animal magnetism as well. Together, Wasikowska and Fassbender are fluent in the poetry of the dialogue and speak it with great fervor. Their performances are fascinating to watch and both have a command over the screen whenever they grace it with their presence. Meanwhile, Dame Judi Dench provides a quiet austerity to her role and gives a quiet performance that is no less impressive than her co-stars. Sally Hawkins, who has seemed incapable of playing a role without a distinct vibrant giddiness, proves that she can indeed inhabit a wretched character. The performances are more than Oscar-worthy, but due to how early Jane Eyre was released in 2011 it will likely be overlooked come voting time. Hopefully theAcademy will avoid such a tragedy and keep this film in the forefront of their mind.    Elegantly is the only adverb that springs to mind when describing how this film was made. The subtle foreboding of the art direction by Karl Probert, the beauty of the cinematography by Adriano Goldman, the lovely music by Dario Marianelli that quietly accentuates each scene, and the complete control over visual style and consistency of tone that comes from director Fukunaga. The film, for two of the five senses, is a beauty to behold. Absolutely dripping with atmosphere, Jane Eyre is one of the best films of the year. A great story, marvelously told, expertly made, and beautifully acted. A gorgeous, compelling period drama that is completely accessible to a modern audience without ever trying to be.    GRADE: A

B00003CXA6|2.0|Many have commented on the historical accuracy of this film.  While the timing of these events themselves may be accurate, i.e., the first days fight, Little Round Top, and Longstreet's Assault (what most call \\"Pickett's Charge), the accuracy of the actual battle sequences leaves much to be desired.  For example, in one scene, Lee is seen riding with several staff members and subordinates and says, \\"To all commanders attack.\\"  This would have taken place during the very late morning or just after noon.  The next scene shows the death of Union General John Reynolds.  Reynolds was shot very early in the mornings fight, long before Rodes' division arrived on the field and the afternoon attacks occured.  Later, Tom Chamberlain is talking with some Confederate prisoners.  First the prisoner says he's from Tennessee, Archer's brigade.  He then says he was captured in the Railroad Cut.  Anyone with even a minimal knowledge of the battle knows it was Joe Davis's Mississippians who were engaged in the RR Cut, Archer's Tennesseans were on the south side of the Chambersberg Pike in McPherson's Woods fighting the Iron Brigade.  And one last inaccuracy I will point out (although there are many others) is Chamberlain and the 20th Maine.  Did they fight valiantly? Yes! Did they save the Union army?  Alone, definately NO!!  They had a large part, but much other valiant fighting occured on Little Round Top.  Also, the famous bayonet charge is way overblown.  Col. William Oates stated he was on the verge of pulling back after 3 hard charges up the hill.  The Alabamians were not making another charge up the hill when the 20th charged down the hill.  Chamberlain led the 20th gallantly, and was an important part of the defense, but other commanders knew how to \\"refuse the line\\" and order \\"counter charges\\"; it was pretty basic tactics and any good colonel would have done the same.    I enjoy the movie as a Civil War movie and love watching it for entertainment.  However, if it's historical accuracy you want, there are many excellent books out there to read.  For history read \\"Gettysburg: The First Day\\"; \\"Gettysburg: The Second Day\\"; \\"Gettysburg: Culp's Hill and Cemetary Hill\\", all by Harry Pfanz.  \\"Picketts Charge\\" by George Stewart is excellent and the reason Pfanz never bothered covering the subject.  And finally, the entire battle is covered very well in books by Stephen Sears and Noah Andre Trudeau.  For Little Round Top see, \\"Stand Firm Ye Boys from Maine\\" by Thomas Desjardin for the 20th Maines action, and/or \\"Twilight at Little Round Top\\" by Glenn W. LaFantasie for the entire action on Little Round Top.    I am open to comments if I am wrong on these observations, but the extensive reading I've done and battlefield tours with Licensed Battlefield Guides (3 hours at Little Round Top) form the basis of observations.    Harold Killinger

076790592X|5.0|Tuesdays With Morrie is a highly-praised non-fiction book, and there is a plethora of reasons for that praise. One, of course, is that the book deals with the challenging subject of the deterioration of physical life, and, finally, death without shying away. Another is that the book invites readers into an intimate relationship, one characterized by unusual warmth and reassuring comfort in a scary situation. But this is a book, after all, and its motive and message are only as good as its writing makeup.<br /><br />This book is about a man named Mitch and his one-of-a-kind college professor, Morrie Schwartz, who reconnect after sixteen years. Mitch visits his old professor, only to see that he is running the last leg of his life, ill with a condition called Amyotrophic Lateral Sclerosis, or Lou Gehrig's disease. Mitch is initially scared and saddened by this news, but he nevertheless visits Morrie every Tuesday unfailingly, learning more about the mind inside the man. During these visits, Morrie teaches the final course of his life - lessons about life and death. These lessons are recounted in the short book, Tuesday by Tuesday, story by story, and are written in the most candid and heartfelt way possible. As a book, Tuesdays With Morrie is a stand-out. The minimal description is powerful; the unaltered conversations are beautiful. Readers are able to dive into the mind of a man who is not in any way ordinary in his outlook towards death. Morrie is a realized figure passing on his words of wisdom, up until the last breath.<br /><br />This book elicits such emotion from its readers; I could feel every tear rolling down my face and soaked up each little story like a sponge. This suffers from some of the same issues and it actually seems to be getting worse in some respects.  The problems I have with this latest book are as follows:<br /><br />1.  One of the biggest things for me is the repetition of certain phrases that were never present before in the setting.  Examples:  \\"much and more\\", \\"little and less\\", and the newest one \\"words are wind\\".  I would be completely fine with these phrases if they were presented as some kind of Essos regional vernacular, but they are not.  Ever since book four, the first two phrases are popping up (seemingly) on every character's lips, all over the world at the same time, when they have never said them before. Also, \\"you know nothing\\" or the new one, \\"where do whores go\\", over and over again...not as bad, because it's just one character using this, but still...ugh!  The editor should have nipped this thing in the bud early.<br /><br />2.  Way too much detail on mundane aspects of everyday life.  I don't want to read paragraphs about what type of food the characters are eating (unless it has some impact on the story) or the drudgery of travel, as it might have been, in the middle ages.  This is, again, something new that has cropped up in the last two books.<br /><br />3.  There also seems to be a new fixation on waste, whether it's a character talking/thinking/seeing \\"nightsoil\\" or, and this one really bugged me, \\"shaking off the last drop\\"...really!?  Sorry, but I don't appreciate that image more than once (and even once, not really).<br /><br />These are all just things that rip me out of the story as I'm happily reading along (well, maybe not happily after book four).<br /><br />In addition, the story, starting from the last book, has lost some of it's excitement for me.  The characters in some ways don't seem to be the same (e.g. Dany seems like a typical modern-day 16-year old girl pining after the \\"bad boy\\" - somebody needs to let her know that she has Dragons...I mean Dragons girlfriend!...Hello!) She didn't act that way before.  It could also just be the plodding along that the characters are currently going through, but it sure would have benefited from a few more \\"fast-forward\\" chapters.<br /><br />This is all very unfortunate as I had recommended this to every one of my reading friends (the first three books are awesome!), and now, I have to step back from that. Here's hoping Mr. Martin listens to his fans, or maybe the editor listens, and these things get fixed for the next books.  It's not like these should be big things for them to fix, but they are big annoyances to me as I'm reading...and there are too many other things competing for my time to waste it on something that annoys me.  Anyway, my two cents...or...actually, my fifteen dollars!  :-(<br /><br />Thanks,<br />Mike
0439023513|4.0|I just read this trilogy, after my son devoured the books and recommended them to me. I love the story, and I would love to give it five stars, but it falls disappointingly short of that award. I'm reminded of a review I read of the television miniseries Holocaust, I don't recall the author, who said \\"it was so good, it's too bad it wasn't a lot better.\\" I understood exactly what he meant. Holocaust told a powerful story, but only sort of powerfully told: there were too many times it was not quite able to escape a \\"made for tv\\" or \\"very special episode\\" feel. Mockingjay is a great story that would have made a great book but it was published a few drafts early.     First, what I love about this story. Mockingjay seems to have garnered the most negative reviews of the three books, and it looks to me like much of that is because it disappoints the expectations of those reading it as a typical romance and/or sci fi fantasy book. But that is part of what I admire most about it. There's nothing wrong with escapist fare, and that can be quite enjoyable and worth reading, but if that is what you are looking for, Mockingjay does not provide it. The romantic angle of \\"Peeta or Gale\\" is overwhelmed by the enormity of war. The typical hero(ine) rising above it all and leading the way to the rescue falls short in the face of the overwhelming task. What this provides instead is a moving realistic look at a heroic young character doing her flawed best in a world far bigger than herself.      I'm reminded here of Vera Brittain's Testament of Youth, her memoir of life as a young English woman during World War I. As the war breaks out, Vera responds to it as an inconvenience to her plans for college and marriage. But she soon learns that, crucial as her relationships with her fianc, brother, and other close friends may be to her, that is but a mere drop in the ocean of war. \\"Peeta or Gale\\" is still an important question to Katniss, and she is still the focus of the book, but the book makes clear that she is a small part of the story, more Audie Murphy's significant but small role as an inspiring heroic fighter rather than a leadership role of Douglas Haig or Woodrow Wilson.     Another common disappointment was the epilogue, a sparse outline that fails to satisfy a desire for a full overcoming of past struggles into an uncomplicatedly happy ending. But the brief sketch provided brought to my mind accounts I have read by and about Holocaust survivors and their children, and the impossibility of ever fully leaving the past behind. I read an interview with Suzanne Collins in which she revealed that her father never got over the nightmares he brought back from the Vietnam War. Again, this book strives for realism, and achieves it admirably.    So, how does it fall short? As I said above, it feels not quite finished. Two examples. Collins uses the \\"knock Katniss unconscious and have someone quickly summarize a lot of action when she wakes up\\" a few too many times. Once is fine. Twice is acceptable if the episode itself is important for the plot. But I wish Collins had put in more time and effort to write Katniss through some of those episodes, unsure of what else was going on, and discovering it later, which is something Collins does a fine job of many other times. It felt to me like cheating for her to resort to that shortcut more than a time or two.     Then there is the rushed end, another common source of disappointment among readers. But it isn't just the story that is rushed; the writing itself seemed rushed. What especially bothers me about the end is that it does a poor job in its attempt to raise questions and provoke thought in the readers. There is certainly nothing wrong with ambiguity and raising questions, indeed that makes, for some readers, for a much more enjoyable book than one which either tells the reader what to think or refrains from challenging the reader to think. But there are better and worse ways to do so. I think here of Dangerous Liaisons. Choderlos de Laclos wrote that book as a collection of letters by and to the characters in the story. The two main characters are maliciously manipulative, bragging to each other about how they have deceived others in the story. The reader cannot help but conclude that they must also be at times deceiving each other, but of course we cannot know for sure when or how. The author uses this technique to magnificent effect in piling layers of ambiguity on each other, provoking unending opportunities for the reader to ponder significant questions.     Collins, however, has presented us with a narrator who, until nearly the end of the trilogy, is reliably, indeed rigorously, honest as a narrator. She is not reliably accurate - she misunderstands situations, she misinterprets others' motives, she often does not understand her own motives. She is not always honest with others in the story. But she is rigorously honest with the reader about her understanding of her experiences. Yet right at the end, she suddenly gets coy with the reader. Katniss is part of a group that must make a significant decision, and she casts a vote that seems quite surprising and out of character (I'm trying to avoid spoilers), yet with only a brief cryptic explanation to the others in the group and absolutely no explanation to the reader. This is not the narrator we have had to this point. When she attends Snow's execution (I don't think I'm giving away anything significant by saying Snow's side loses), she suddenly switches her actions at a crucial moment and again fails even to attempt an explanation to the reader. This does raise some interesting questions to contemplate, but it is totally out of character for the narrative structure. Collins certainly hints; but, completely counter to what we have had to this point, Katniss does not tell. I wish Collins had spent more time trying to devise other ways to raise these questions to fit the structure of the story. She made gooduse of, for example, Katniss's youth and her lack of access to the inner circles of power elsewhere to raise ambiguities and provoke questions in the reader. Perhaps, though, the questions Collins wanted to raise here could not be raised within that structure. If that is the case, she should have restructured the narration (though that would be difficult given that the first two books had already been published) or raised different questions.     But in spite of these flaws, I loved this story and found the series well worth reading. It was so good, I couldn't help but be a bit disappointed it wasn't a lot better.

It's possible to follow the 80s lovefest, but not so interesting to someone who's more familiar with the Ducktail than the Mullet. There was a good story buried in Ready Player One, but I found myself skimming -- a lot -- to get to it.

B000E1ZBGS|1.0|The most basic description of Jane Austen's book, \\"Pride and Prejudice\\", is that it is a humorous, romantic satire about the hypocrisy of class distinctions among the landed gentry. That is to say, it's about \\"rich\\" vs. \\"poor\\" (where the poor aren't really all that poor). In England, in the early 1800's--the setting of this book--there was a great deal wrong with system of money and property transfer, which was very patriarchal, and terribly unfair to women, and the behavior of the so-called elite towards those who weren't fortunate enough to be born into wealth. Part of the humor and genius of P&P is how refined, polite and kind the lower-class Jane and Elizabeth behave as compared to Lady Catherine, Miss Bingley, and Mr. Collins, all of whom possess all the appearance of class while being as rude and insulting as possible. Jane Austen was making a tart social statement as well as delivering a heady romance.<br /><br />So what on earth is going on in this film? The Bennets aren't so much landed gentry as flat-out peasants. They all behave abominably. The language isn't playful and dripping with hidden meaning; it's ultra-modernized and dumbed down. As far as the total reinterpretation of the story, I am guessing that the writer and director don't even know what this story is all about or threw it aside in favor of making this into a bodice-ripper.  Mr. Darcy, a member of the highest possible social rank, might look down on Elizabeth as a potential wife for being born to a family with less money, but there is NO way that a man of his stature would EVER propose to someone who lives in squalor and has no manners, as this Elizabeth is portrayed. It's as if they were all blowsy Americans, as opposed to stiff-upper-lip Britons.<br /><br />As far as the performances go, the actors here are dreadful. Keira Knightley is the same character in every single movie she's done--either open mouthed and wide-eyed like a deer in the headlights, or with a pinched and vacant smile. Matthew McFayden has as much range as a saltine cracker. The rest of the Bennet girls are indistinguishable from one another, and in fact, I kept getting Charlotte confused for one of them, because they all looked and acted identical to one another. There was wayyyy too much giggling.<br /><br />The period details are all out of whack. Why is Lady Catherine wearing a hairstyle that was popular around the time she would have been born? Why does Caroline look like a character out of a contemporary sitcom? The whole fast-and-loose approach with the styles, mannerisms, and language of the period make it impossible to mentally inhabit the period they're portraying. The behavior, language, clothing and set design are so completely wrong for the period that the only way this film would work would be as science fiction. They should have avoided doing a period piece at all.as as much range as a saltine cracker. B002T9H2L0|5.0|This movie may not be Tarantino's most artful, star-studded, or controversial film. But I found it to be perhaps his most watchable. The great irony is that the film is full of long dialogue scenes that shy away from Tarantino's usual hipster vernacular, but nonetheless remain laced with wit, polish, and the sheer joy of verbal communication. It does not seem possible that so many extended scenes could exist in one movie, yet each one seems to be the very height of tension. It seems someone always has something at stake: hidden identities, hidden agendas, hidden Jew dairy farmers. The film takes its time, stretching the characters' nerves to the breaking point before finally moving on to the next episode. It's an entrancing bit of work.<br /><br />As usual, Tarantino has a knack for casting. Chrisoph Waltz as an evil Nazi brings a breathtaking sense of joy to his work. This is no one dimensional villain, and his Oscar is every bit deserved. Brad Pitt, as leader of the Basterds, has the most outrageous Tennessee accent ever heard in a WWII movie, and manages to be hilarious every time he steps on stage. The cast is uniformly awesome all down the line.<br /><br />This is one of those movies I've watched again and again on Blu-Ray, because I find the sheer effortlessness of it to be fascinating. It has its fair share of violence, language, and the like, but none of those seem as egregious as past Tarantino efforts. Perhaps because this is his first period film that doesn't take place in the 70s (or pseudo-70s). This movie is in fact based on one of those old B-Movies from the 70s, but once again Quentin proves himself capable of taking back-alley source material and elevating it to fine art.
B004EPZ08O|2.0|The Review:<br /><br />It's another Final Destination movie. Along with the Saw franchise, this is one of Hollywood's old standbys that's ridden a pretty original idea into the ground-well not all the way into the ground. It still manages to be fun as long as you manage your expectations.<br /><br />The Final Destination formula is the same. Right before a major disaster -- think plane crash, roller coaster, huge traffic accident -- the protagonist has a vivid premonition of things to come. Right after he or she sees their own death they snap back to a point right before the disaster and warn their friends to get off of, or out of whatever, or wherever the disaster is about to strike. The twist is that death doesn't like to be cheated and through a series of gory Rube Goldberg like accidents, it will claim the lives of anyone who escaped the initial disaster. The protagonist and his circle of friends take a little while to catch on, but inevitably the series of improbable deaths of everyone who escaped the initial disaster clues the dwindling survivors in. At this point some contrivance is discovered by which the remaining survivors might be able to cheat death.<br /><br />I've just described the plot of every Final Destination film, which is kind of why I'll never recommend you watch any of the later films for the storytelling. The premise of the Final Destination films was fresh until the second sequel. Final Destination is now about one thing and one thing only. That is the insane, weird, and horrible ways in which the cast is killed off. It is a fundamentally shallow movie going experience. There is very little character development, and no proper villain, unless a concept like death can be a villain. This lack of a villain has always made the Final Destination films less about horror than about shock value. Paranormal Activity, The Blair Witch and Ring had moments of real terror. Final Destination relies exclusively on shock value. It's a subtle difference, but why, at least in this viewer's opinion, it's not going to rank up there with movies like the aforementioned Paranormal Activity or the more recent Insidious.<br /><br />Now, while this review has not been glowing, I did in fact have a good time with Final Destination 5. B00081MUY0|5.0|The title track, which opens the album, is well known and sounds just as fresh today as when it was recorded. Donovan, previously labelled by some as an imitator of Bob Dylan, stunned the music world with this piece, which is as far removed from folk as one could get.    There is however far more to this album. The pace changes completely with track 2, \\"Legend of a Girl Child Linda\\", a sweet fairytale that I think goes on a trifle too long, but nevertheless shows Donovan's ability to create beauty out of utter simplicity.    For me, things really get going with the next song, \\"Three King Fishers\\", which sets the mood that permeates the rest of the album - that mood being moody and contemplative, slightly brooding and menacing. Heavy Eastern influence can be heard on this track, as well as several others, with the use of traditional Indian instruments such as sitar. There is a hypnotic quality to these songs -  Three King Fishers, Ferris Wheel and Guinevere - that allows the listener to drift into a dark fantasy world. In between we have the bluesy \\"Bert's Blues\\", the straight pop-rock of \\"Season of the Witch\\" and \\"The Trip\\", another bluesy number that name checks a selection of characters, real and fictional, including Bob Dylan and Joan Baez. \\"The Fat Angel\\" is another piece of Eastern-influenced psychedelia; the title refers to Mama Cass Elliott,  and Jefferson Airplane also crack a mention. All these avant-garde influences are set aside for the final track, \\"Celeste\\", which is a simple love ballad and the perfect closer for the album.    The bonus tracks are also worthwhile. It's especially interesting to hear an alternative version of \\"Superlungs\\", which surfaced on a later album as \\"Superlungs My Supergirl\\". The instrumentation in the version here is quite different, and is strangely reminiscent of \\"Light My Fire\\" by The Doors.    This album is one that I point to when I want to indicate to people why the late 60s were such a special period in music.

0316098329|4.0|Room by Emma Donoghue was one of the most talked about books of 2010. 0743297334|2.0|Although I have a house full of books, bursting with books, book shelves in every room, piles of books on a chest of drawers by the back door waiting for new shelves to be built, books along the edges of steps leading to the second floor, stacks of books in the master bedroom waiting their turn to be read...  I oftentimes find that the book I am reading whispers the name of the next book, or next several books, rather than I having the luxury to choose for myself. This was definitely the case with \\"The Paris Wife\\", the subject of my last review.  I discovered while I was devouring it that when living in Paris at the beginning of his writing career, Ernest Hemingway became enamored with Bull fighting in Spain. He went to Pamplona several times to take in the contests, after first being introduced to them through stories told by Gertrude Stein (a friend of Hemingway's who was also an ex-pat living in Paris, and who also became a famous author).  In fact Hemingway's first child was named John Hadley Nicanor Hemingway.  Nicanor Villalta was the name of a famous toreador who artfully slay his bull the first time Hemingway took his wife, Hadley, to the fights while she was pregnant with their son.  Hemingway's first novel and work of substance, as well as the subject of this review, was \\"The Sun Also Rises\\", a story woven around and through the passion Hemingway felt for bullfighting.    In 1926, while Hemingway was living in Paris, he took his wife and a group of friends to Spain for the week long festival.  There were seven people in the party, mostly Americans familiar with each other from living in Paris and traveling in the same social circle.  There was one other woman besides Hemingway's wife in the group, Lady Duff Twysden, who had once upon a time in one of her previous unions, married into her title.  She went by Duff.  She was beautiful, glamorous, and made it a point of attracting the attention of the men around her in spite of the fact that she was engaged tobe married to another member of the party, Pat Guthrie.  Pat drank too much and didn't hold his liquor well, but even so he was cognizant of the mutual flirtations that were going on around him involving his fiancee.  To add to this amorously charged and stress-filled atmosphere, another member of the group, Harold Loeb, had only just returned from a romantic tryst at St.-Jean-de-Luz with Duff.  Harold was head-over-heels for Duff, and felt that after having her to himself for a week, he had a legitimate claim to her.  She was finished with Harold by then in her off-hand way, and had moved on to the attentions of Hemingway...  During the course of the week, Hemingway, Harold and Pat circled Duff with the intensity of sharks at a feeding frenzy, which created an aura of discomfort for all of the friends.    The \\"Sun Also Rises\\" takes place in Pamplona during the bullfighting festival in July. It is actually based on the trip Hemingway planned for his friends.  Interestingly Hemingway chose not to include his wife in the novel, even though she was present during the actual event.  In the book there is continuous inebriation, arguing, and fighting over the favor of Brett, who plays the part of Duff.  The twist is that Brett trifles with the men, but sets her sights on conquering the beautiful young toreador after she sees the grace, power and fearlessness of his performance in the ring.    It was interesting to read this book after having an understanding for Hemingway's life when he wrote it.  It was also fascinating to know that Hemingway based this book on a real life experience.  It was like having a window into his existence. Unfortunately, I can't honestly say I enjoyed his writing style.  I know it is said of Hemingway that the power of his writing came from his ability to be succinct.  I found, however, that his writing lacked the richness of description and analogy, which made it feel flat.  He also repeated words and phrases, which I thought tedious.  I am keeping in mind that this was Hemingway's first novel, and that he was not yet a literary giant when he wrote it.  I am looking forward to reading \\"A Movable Feast\\", which was the last book Hemingway wrote before his death.  It is a reminiscence of his life in Paris with Hadley.

1608191664|5.0|All too often in economics, as well as other social sciences, theories backed by frequently repeated narratives become law. The burden of proof just becomes less as time goes on and the public begins to internalize erroneous assumptions as fact. Ha-Joon Chang turns economic \\"common sense\\" on its head by introducing neglected histories and phenomenal quantitative comparisons. He dismantles the old knowledge regime while giving the reader a new appreciation for non-free-market forces, although as you will learn, there is no \\"free\\"-market (see chapter/thing one).    The book takes a peak at 23 obscured facts regarding market economics and the global economy. Almost all 23 will come as a surprise to those new to globalization critisism. For those who have Chang's last book, Bad Samaritiams, this book has a similar feel and is every bit as informative. Most of the information presented runs counterintuitive to widely held beliefs on free-markets, but Chang provides ample evidence through empirical analysis to support his findings.     Besides being an incredibly valuable book for any student of economics, it is also a very fast read given the set-up of the chapters (6-8 pages long); I got the book on Tuesday afternoon and I'm writing this review after finishing on Thursday evening of the same week (granted I've been voraciously reading since I opened it.) The text is very diverse in its potential uses; it could be used to supplement course material for a class or just as a casual read for someone trying to get a better grip on reality during these tumultuous economic times. In any event, Ha-Joon Chang has made a great contribution to the economic knowledge pool with this book!

B0050I2P32|4.0|It was John Peel who once complained about too many \\"white boys with guitars\\" in indie music and it has to be said that this pointed taunt could have been accurately aimed at the Horrors whose claim to fame was largely being the best set of Goth haircuts on offer and little else. As such it was refreshing to hear the massive step change that was their last album \\"Primary Colours\\" which saw them cut loose and prove that the style did not have to triumph over the substance, They picked up influences from German bands like Can and Neu and turned out big songs not least the epic \\"Sea within a sea\\". That said while \\"Primary Colours\\" was a really good album it still had \\"issues\\" as the band struggled to find its true identity with songs like \\"I can't control myself\\" actually sounding like a bunch of Suede impersonators on Karaoke night. The logical step for the band was therefore to deepen further the themes of \\"Sea within a sea\\" and produce a more darkly inspired album of 80s style synth pop with hooks big enough to catch a whale and with cool panache to spare. Indeed they do this so successfully that on the third track on this new album \\"I can see through you starts\\" you expect Phil Oakley from the Human League to sing out just before it mutates into something altogether more sinister.<br /><br />There is little doubt that some of the more purist Horrors fans may recoil and find this album a tad to commercial. \\"Endless Blue\\" for example is all lovely slabs of almost Miles Davis trumpet sounding synths until mid way through it breaks into a huge guitar riff not heard since Jesus and Mary Chain ruled the earth. And that is the point about \\"Skying\\" namely that the Horrors are a cunningly intelligent band able to subvert and deconstruct their own songs regularly avoiding the quiet of the harbour for the rough of the seas. More than that unlike \\"Primary colours\\" the quality dial hardly dips while at the same time the sort of arrogant swagger that you expect of a band hitting its prime is evidenced by the truckload. The great opener \\"Changing the rain\\" starts off sounding like a dance version of Kraftwerk and we are finally forced to face the reality that Faris Badwan has actually got a great singing voice. The forthcoming single \\"Still life\\" cries out to be a hit building from (dare I say it!) an Orchestral Manoeuvres in the Dark style riff to a thumping great pop song. Above all others it is \\"Wide eyed\\" which is the song that grabs you by the lapels and demands your attention. An atmospheric vocal over swaths of synths which echoes the great themes of David Bowie's \\"Low\\". Even more surprisingly it is a very sumptuous song with huge melodies and a concluding trumpet fade out. The only real nod to the Horrors of old is \\"Monica Gems\\" which is the weakest track on \\"Skying\\" not least since it does feel a little out of place with Faris Badwan again doing his best Brett Anderson impression. To be fair they set this misstep straight on the final track the seven minute plus \\"Oceans Burning\\" a giant swirling psychedelic ballad with a haunting backdrop which is packed full of so many ideas that the opposition must be seriously worried quite how good this band have become. To hammer the point home all this is confirmed a couple of tracks before with the albums big eight minute synth workout \\"Moving further away\\" which will become a staple of their live act to sit alongside \\"The Sea\\" with a keyboard riff so relentless you should be allowed to invoke anti-stalking legislation. \\"Skying\\" then is an great album that fully delivers on the huge promise of \\"Primary Colours\\" and on its evidence it is only a matter of time before the Horrors move out of the John Peel tent and head to the headline stage of Glastonbury.ime is evidenced by the truckload. The old saying \\"When you hear hoof beats, don't assume zebras\\" may apply here. For my part, I've bought a couple more books from Amazon on the subject, which I haven't had time to read yet, although I doubt they will be anymore convincing than Journey of Souls. 0307716511|3.0|\\"Lord help the mister who comes between me and my sister...\\"<br /><br />Or to paraphrase Shakespeare: \\"Hell hath no fury like a sister scorned.\\"  Or grieving, as the case may be.  In \\"Sister,\\" Rosamund Lupton's novel cum mystery, sibling affection is taken to the nth degree, as a woman, Beatrice, sets out to find the person who murdered her art student sister.  Not that anyone believes it was murder, mind you.  As far as the police and everyone else are concerned, Tess has committed suicide after the death of her baby during childbirth.  Only Bee believes that something more sinister is afoot, and sets out to prove everyone wrong.<br /><br />If the precis above sounds cliched, that's because it is.  But author Lupton, whose debut this is, manages to fool us into thinking she's doing something new by virtue of some lovely writing, some modern plot elements (the ethics of genetic testing)  and a marvelous ability to create suspense.  The familial bond is also explored (and described) with great sensitivity and truth.  Bee's grief (as well as her mother's) is vivid, palpable; so much so that we truly empathize with her in spite of her uptight fussiness and judgmental nature.  How loss changes Bee, how it opens her up as a human being, is one of the books most successfully explored themes.<br /><br />What is not successful, unfortunately, is the book's last third.  What was earlier suspenseful (the narrative moving between past and present) comes to feel like an author treading water.  Lupton seems to get bored with the puzzle she's constructed, and the tension slacks and dissipates.  The book is 30 to 40 pages too long, which allows us as readers to get ahead of Lupton, so that the surprise ending is really no surprise.  (With the exception of a pointless, last-minute twist/shocker that adds nothing to our understanding of the story.)<br /><br />Many of the reviews have praised \\"Sister\\" for its literary qualities, and Lupton is, indeed, a terrific writer.  Sometimes I felt that the prose was a bit purple given that this is a first-person narrative (who of us thinks in perfectly constructed metaphors and similes?).  But, what I initially found artificial and precious (Bee communing with her dead sister to explain how she caught her killer), I soon took for granted.  Bee's \\"writing\\" to Tess became a shared comfort, and quickly seemed the only sensible way in which the book could have been written.  Only a gifted writer could pull off the conceit.  Who knows if Lupton will continue to write in the mystery genre?  If she does, here's hoping her plotting will tighten, and that she'll learn that twists are only of value if they illuminate as well as surprise.  I look forward to reading what she comes up with next.en that this is a first-person narrative (who of us thinks in perfectly constructed metaphors and similes?). B004LWZVZC|5.0|The Conspirator, directed with skill by Robert Redford, is a sleek and atmospheric historical drama.  The story is meticulously researched and suspenseful throughout.  In the 145 years since the actual event, many theories have developed on the guilt or innocence of Mary Surratt, the boardinghouse owner whose son John was almost certainly part of the plot to assassinate Abraham Lincoln, his Vice President Andrew Johnson and his Secretary of State William H. Seward.<br /><br />Many historians now agree that Mary Seward was probably part of the conspiracy.  Although she may not have taken part in the actual planning, she almost certainly knew and approved of John Wilkes Booth's plot against the government and, had she not approved, she probably would have been able to stop it.  The focus of this film is neither her innocence or guilt, but rather her lack of a fair trial, given that the Secretary of War, Edward M. Stanton, not only directed that the conspirators receive trial by a military tribunal, he was later denounced by many as tampering with witnesses during the trial.  The Supreme Court later ruled that the trial of citizens by military tribunal was unconstitutional.  Mary Surratt's son John, a courier and Confederate spy who was also probably guilty but was not apprehended until 1866, was given a trial by jury and released after his trial resulted in a hung jury.<br /><br />However, the most impressive aspect of this film, aside from its predominantly historical accuracy, is the amazing amount of care that went into its visuals.  Costumes, sets, and every other physical aspect of the story are brought to life down to the smallest detail, with a look and atmosphere that makes the viewer feel they have stepped back into the middle of the 19th century.  The historic photos that were taken of the execution of the four main conspirators were the obvious source of design for the hanging scene and one has to marvel at how authentic it all looks by every account of the actual story.  The scene of the trial and execution looks precisely like the Washington arsenal where it all took place, and so do the scenes in Ford's theatre, the Surratt boardinghouse and virtually every other location used in the making of this wonderful film.<br /><br />Included with the DVD is a second disc that includes a full-length documentary, with fascinating facts that I was previously unaware of, and I am a Lincoln buff.  I wish only that the film had included a little more on the attempted assassination of William H. Seward; the fact that he survived is not evident in the scene where he is attacked, although at the trial, Seward's attacker is referred to several times as the \\"attempted assassin\\".<br /><br />On the whole, The Conspirator is an extremely satisfying and vividly detailed historical drama, and is sure to be enjoyed by anyone who is interested in the American Civil War or the Assassination of Abraham Lincoln.<br /><br />Highly recommended.The scene of the trial and execution looks precisely like the Washington arsenal where it all took place, and so do the scenes in Ford's theatre, the Surratt boardinghouse and virtually every other location used in the making of this wonderful film. Seward; the fact that he survived is not evident in the scene where he is attacked, although at the trial, Seward's attacker is referred to several times as the \\"attempted assassin\\".    On the whole, The Conspirator is an extremely satisfying and vividly detailed historical drama, and is sure to be enjoyed by anyone who is interested in the American Civil War or the Assassination of Abraham Lincoln.       Highly recommended.

1439183392|4.0|In Winslow's world, nothing is ever as simple as it seems. Set in Pacific Beach in San Diego County, California, familiar characters return from The Dawn Patrol, a group of dedicated early morning PB surfers whose nicknames cling to them like the surf stories of their popular mythology. While corporate interests and drug cartels inbreed with troubling ease, the guys of the Dawn Patrol find solace in the ocean that has given them such grand adventures and camaraderie over the years. Unfortunately for Boone Daniels, veteran of the Dawn Patrol and PI dabbling in shifting allegiance to \\"The Gentleman's Hour\\", the line between old alliances and the demands of the future set him apart from the once-inviolable brotherhood. Defense attorney Petra Hall has requested Boone's assistance with a murder case, the tragic death of a revered surfer and Boone's mentor, Kelly Kuhio. The guilt of Corey Blasingame isn't in doubt, but the severity of the murder charge is. Others were involved- Corey's buddies- in a random, perhaps racially-motivated attack that ended Kuhio's life.<br /><br />This scenario is pure Winslow, salted with surf jargon and the social intricacies of a strip of coastline impacted by drug money, wealthy residents and a cadre of men who have cut their teeth on the rush of wave riding in the Pacific Ocean, a little strip of heaven where you don't have to be rich to feel like a king, surrounded by a vast and powerful body of water, far removed from the daily grind of disappointing lives and fading youth, where surf and friends create their own version of paradise. It doesn't help that Daniels is deeply attracted to the British Petra, the siren call of a solid relationship as seductive as the habit of hanging out at the Sundowner after a hard day's surfing, or Boone's instinctive retreat to his bungalow on the pier, surrounded by the calming rhythm of the waves.<br /><br />Winslow mines his usual heady blend of criminal enterprise, drug violence, white-collar profiteering, romance, surf and murder, acutely conscious of the knife-edged dynamic of greed vs. the environment, the lure of money, the California coast and a history steeped in love of nature's bounty. Ever contemporary, Winslow never veers into sensationalism or trickery, calls out a situation exactly as it is and explores the fragile connections of friendship and loyalty, a band of brothers forged into a distinct cultural phenomenon: surfers. Enter the woman- and the future- and Daniels is forced to leave the security of the familiar and do the right thing. But you can bet he does it with style. A unique voice in crime literature, Winslow embodies the coastal gestalt, irreverent, fearless, a little cynical and a lot pragmatic and very worldly-wise. B004I9TQLI|4.0|I've read the book upon which this movie is based, and also reviewed it. The book makes for a thoughtful discussion on the Holocaust, though it did have some problems having to do with plot implausibilities. Yet despite all of that, I did think the book added some value to novels dealing with the Holocaust, especially in portraying the story from the 8-year-old German Aryan boy's point of view.<br /><br />Now on to the movie - the story follows the book closely with some adaptations. Set during WW II, 8-year-old German Aryan, Bruno finds his carefree life in Berlin is about to come to an end as his father [David Thelis] has been promoted to the position of Kommandant thus necessitating the family's move to a rural outpost which is nameless. So, Bruno [Asa Butterfield], his 12-year-old sister Gretel [Amber Beattie], mother [Vera Farmiga] and dad make the move. Bruno is initially not impressed with his new surroundings - there are no other houses nearby and the children are not able to attend school, having to be taught by an old tutor who very much subscribes to Nazi ideology, pummeling the children with his ideology of hatred every chance he gets. Young Bruno in the meantime has discovered a 'farm' close by and to his immense delight, meets another 8-year-old boy with the curious name of Schmuel [Jack Scanlon] who seems to have tons of 'friends' and has the luxury of wearing 'pajamas' all day. Though he is forbidden to explore the back of the house, Bruno makes clandestine visits to his friend, who sadly cannot play with him as they are separated by a barbed wire fence, which Bruno thinks is to keep the animals out. The pairs friendship grows every day, and Bruno begins to discover certain things - that Jews are not treated well [he doesn't really buy the \\"Jews are evil\\" idea], and begins to see his father in a different light [the Kommandant keeps silent when a Jewish houseworker is pummeled by a sadistic Nazi underling]. In the meantime, Bruno's mother Ilse [Vera Farmiga] begins to fall apart as she comes to discover what really goes on in the camps [extermination of Jews] and suffers an emotional breakdown. The rest of the story deals with what happens to Bruno and Schmuel and the others.<br /><br />There are some plot implausibilities - the fact that Bruno is so incredibly naive about Jews and the ideology of hatred against them is hard to accept if only because most German Aryan boys of Bruno's age would have been in the Hitler Youth and thus exposed to such teachings. Bruno being a high-ranking Nazi officer's son would most certainly have been in the Hitler Youth as would his sister. Also, Ilse not knowing of what was going on in the camp [extermination] also seems implausible. As for Schmuel, most young children were immediately selected for the gas chambers upon arrival, and it seems almost miraculous that Schmuel survived for as long as he did, and was able to meet Bruno so often without detection.<br /><br />But, implausibilities aside, I still liked this movie - it manages to portray the innocence of children in a depraved world so very credibly, and this is largely due to Asa Butterfield's amazing performance as the naive Bruno whose eyes are slowly 'opened' to the cruelty evident around him. Jack Scanlon's Schmuel is also credible -the chemistry between the two boys is very real, and their friendship is altogether believable, even the part when Bruno 'betrays' his friend and Schmuel's reaction to this. Vera Farmiga's performance as the tormented Ilse is the most powerful adult performance in this movie - she delivers a finely-nuanced performance that makes us believe Ilse was truly horrified by what was being done to the Jews. The other adult male characters delivered rather weak performances - David Thewlis as the Kommandant was stiff in his role and seemed ill at ease for the most part, the sadistic Nazi driver seemed very much a one-dimensional character, and even the Jewish houseworker was not given much character development.<br /><br />All in all, \\"Boy in the Striped Pajamas\\" succeeds in getting under one's skin and doesn't cop out with a happily ever after ending, for the climax is brutal and mind-numbing. The child actors' powerful performances enables the movie to address questions about humanity and man's capacity for evil.in all, \\"Boy in the Striped Pajamas\\" succeeds in getting under one's skin and doesn't cop out with a happily ever after ending, for the climax is brutal and mind-numbing. The child actors' powerful performances enables the movie to address questions about humanity and man's capacity for evil.

B002ZHKZD8|5.0|Who are you? Where do you come from? Where are you going? The Bourne Ultimatum can be summed up neatly under the following headings: fast paced, action packed, non-stop, thoughtful, and above all: efficient. The key reason why this film works so well is because the film itself is just as sharp as the main character: Jason Bourne. The Bourne Ultimatum is the third installment in the Borne Trilogy. The Bourne Trilogy is a series of novels by Robert Ludlum, which have been adapted to film in a series that include the following three films: The Bourne Identity (2002), The Bourne Supremacy (2004), and The Bourne Ultimatum (2007). This series of high tech thrillers redefined action movies for the 21st century. On the acting stage the Bourne Trilogy is driven by none other then Matt Damon playing Jason Bourne. On the surface this is essentially the story of a man trying to figure out his identity. To accomplish this task Bourne will have to conduct a world wide battle with the forces that created him: the CIA. The intriguing element here is that there is always something else going on. The audience is never completely filled in and always learning something new. The great thing about this entry in the series is that it never really stops from beginning to end. The story is constantly unraveling and you never really know what is going to happen next. Also the story arc is always changing and never sticks to formula. 5558765283|5.0|If you're new to the series and getting to the book for the first time, perhaps having started after seeing the HBO show, take a moment to consider how the previous book ended. Momentous events, some hopeful and some intensely tragic. Several of the POVs are now dealing with the aftermath of what happened in the previous three books and the costs of all that action is now given time to sink in.<br /><br />I was actually quite disappointed when, following A Storm of Swords, we were told that the action would jump ahead five years between books 3 and 4. I felt it would not give readers the time to mourn the deaths and reflect on the events in the previous books since it would be \\"old history\\" for the characters when we next met them.<br /><br />Given this, I was very pleased to see that the jump ahead was abandoned and that we instead were allowed to follow along as the characters dealt with the events of the prior books. I also feel that GRRM's writing is at its most beautiful and poignant in many of the chapters in A Feast for Crows.<br /><br />Of course, some POVs don't appear at all in this book, and I do think that announcement should probably have been at the front of the book instead of at the end; it is frustrating to wait for a chapter that never shows up and not all readers have the benefit of Internet discussions to inform them in advance. So, do yourself a favour and don't turn page after page waiting for Dany, Jon and Tyrion. You'll see them in the next book.<br /><br />There are several new POVs, some fairly brief, but don't look at them as taking up room that your missing favourite could have had. Look at them as more opportunities of seeing new facets of the story. Its all a giant mosaic and all the pieces matter for the final image. That said, I think one really doesn't have to enjoy all POVs to enjoy the overall story; I definitely have my favourites as well as those that I rarely reread.<br /><br />This is, without a doubt, an even more character-driven book than the previous, but this change of tone and pace is something the series has been building towards, with chapters growing longer and more detailed over the previous books. Some readers are clearly disappointed with the change, but others (myself included) have embraced it. But I don't think it is surprising that a series that hooked readers by being different and defying expectations might lose some of them along the way by continuing to be different and continuing to defy expectations.<br /><br />That has nothing to do with a lack of quality, its all down to personal opinion. 1400052181|3.0|I picked up this book after getting recommendations from several friends and finding the subject matter interesting. The book documents the development of the first \\"immortal\\" cell line from the cervical cancer cells of a black woman in the 1950s. The cells were taken without her or her family's knowledge or consent, and led to the development of a multi-billion dollar industry without any compensation for her descendants.      The book is simply written and reduces the science it describes to easily understandable text. I think too much of the book is devoted to the research the author did to find the story (such as how many phone calls she had to make, how many times people hung up on her or didn't show up for interviews, etc.) But the story is an important one, and sheds light on the practice of medicine at that time, the patronizing attitudes of doctors and researchers and the lower class treatment routinely meted out to the black population. The author also describes the horrible mistreatment of blacks in mental institutions at that time, a shameful part of U.S. history.    It's not the highest quality writing, and I would have liked to see more in depth coverage of the ethical issues still lingering in cell research today. However, I do recommend this book as the story is a compelling one. And I admire the author's dogged pursuit of the truth and the respect she accorded the Lacks family throughout.

0307454541|3.0|As a crime mystery, The Girl with the Dragon Tatto is the exactly kind of book that provides just enough suspense as a diversion for a couple of days...and then you forget about it. If you're the kind of person with an entire bookshelf full of airport novels, you'll love it. Others may be a bit more apathetic, having read it for the sake of tuning in to the hype.<br /><br />The dialogue is probably it's biggest weakness, as some of the lines will make you wince, and there isn't much character development going on since most of the effort is spent on cultivating the whodunit. As is the case with a lot of crime fiction, there aren't any character arcs. Larsson doesn't develop Blomqvuist at all and we only have HINTS at who Salander really is. In other words, the characters manage to solve most of the extrapersonal conflicts they face in the story, but none of the inner personal conflicts because there either aren't any presented to solve or they're only HINTED at. While I realize character development was never the hallmark of crime fiction, you'd think there'd be room for more character than what we wind up with in 600 pages. I'm assuming Larsson left Salander a mystery to make her seem \\"cooler.\\" I'm also assuming Larsson wanted to make Blomqvist cool by randomly sleeping with everybody.<br /><br />The second problem I had were the overarching feminist themes (you know, most men in power are sociopaths who hate women and most women are blameless innocent victims struggling against repression in a violent patriarchal society). As an offshoot of these embedded overtones, we get a lot of stereotypical male and female characters that feminist fantasy has indulged and nutured for decades. Larsson no doubt had this in mind when creating Salander--the badass loner renegade chick wronged by men who knows martial arts, carries weapons, wears leather, and engages in high level espionage. This may have been sexy, unique and even interesting at one time; it isn't anymore. You could turn on almost any tv program or movie these days and see the exact same recycled character running around trying to save the world and stop crime.<br /><br />In any case, while it may not be something anybody's ever going to hang up in the pantheon of great literature, it does accomplish more than many other completely overhyped books have failed miserably to do. Three stars, but not convincingly.any tv program or movie these days and see the exact same recycled character running around trying to save the world and stop crime. 1401207529|5.0|This is probably one of the best stories, not just Batman tales, but one of the best STORIES, period, I have read. This is a modern redo of Batman's origins that also intertwines his rise with Lieutenant James Gordon's slow rise to the post of Commissioner (by the end of Year One he is a Captain). The main storyline revolves around Bruce Wayne returning from 18 years of training and traveling across the world after the murder of his parents to start his \\"quest\\" and James Gordon moving from the Chicago PD to Gotham's police force, which, unsurprisingly, is pretty corrupt (A one page prologue drawn as a Gotham newspaper has an article that sets the tone for this). Slowly, since they both are basically working towards the same goal, the start to move toward each other until they finally cross paths. By the end they have developed a working relationship of sorts, and have started to tackle the mundane but powerful elements of crime and corruption in Gotham City; the Mafia, their business partners, and many of Gotham's government officials. I particularly liked the fact that this story does look at what inspired Bruce Wayne to use a bat as a symbol,  and how Jim Gordon became such a tough cop. Outside of the Nolan Batman films, this is probably the most strictly speaking realistic Batman you will see, with all of the human flaws and mistake-making that tend to define people. This also applies to Gordon, who has his own weakness and mistakes to overcome as well. We also see Harvey Dent already have a working relationship with Batman early on as an Assistant D.A. before the addition of Gordon to the team, which adds to his later, tragic character as Two-Face. This is a story you could read twice in a row; I would definitely recommend reading this!    Sidenotes: This story is the current foundation story which is used as the jumping off point for most of the modern Batman comics, and about 10 or so other stories have been written that take place within Year One, rightafter Year One, or parallel to it. A good follow up story is Batman: Shaman, which goes into deeper detail as to why Bruce Wayne seized on the bat as a symbol, and looks at what some of his training entailed. Alternatively, given the hint at the end, an additional good follow up would be Batman: The Man Who Laughs, which introduces the Joker after his accident, and Batman:Dead to Rights, which depicts the Joker's trial after the Man Who Laughs. I should also mention that there is a notable subplot in Year One that involves the origin of Catwoman throughout the main story, which serves to show how Batman, to some degree, inspires or creates his super-villain enemies, and how similar he is to them. Additionally, establishes that Catwoman and Batman are very similar people as well(and could be a couple, thus starting their weird relationship). I should also point out that about 30 or so pages of bonus materials are included in the book, and the story itself is 96 pages (97 counting the \\"newspaper\\"). The cover for the paperback is very nice and has a plastic coating that helps keep it looking fresh.       Cons: I noticed that although most of the reviews are positive, there are a few very negative ones, so I will attempt to list potential reasons why people might not find this story appealing: A. It does not have any colorful or fantastic supervillains (unless you count Catwoman), since Frank Miller was going for a realistic angle.     B. The story does not have a typical one-off plot where a villain is introduced, schemes, and is defeated or escapes; instead it is focused on origins and the development of the characters.This means that the story is a little deeper and longer and takes a bit more effort to follow than a typical comic book storyline.     C. The art is good, but the colors used are chiefly a lot of blues, greys, blacks, reds, oranges and yellows(mainly for the background, not the characters themselves) to set a gritty \\"tone\\", so those who prefer a somewhat more colorful comic or are comic art critics or hard core comic fans may be disappointed. The average reader, like myself, should not find this to be an issue.     The only issue I had with my copy was that there were slight marks from shipping, but since this has nothing to do with the enjoyment of the product, I give it 5 stars.

And now for something completely different...<br /><br /> Both brutal and emotionally delicate, this fairy tale-driven movie follows a young, female, elven-looking Jason Bourne in her journey to defeat the wicked witch.  Hanna tries to appeal to both art house critics and action movie connoisseurs, but succeeds considerably more in the ethereal former than the latter.<br /><br />Hanna begins very slowly, but with no shortage of intrigue, as we are introduced to Hanna (Saoirse Ronan) and her father (Eric Bana) living in some subarctic wilderness.  They make ends meet by living off the grid with zero human interaction and hunting for self-sufficiency.  Like any father concerned for his early-teenage daughter, he randomly assaults her to test her developing martial prowess--for which she has a frightening facility.  Near bedtime Hanna is drilled to answer questions in Spanish, Italian, German...no problem.  We later learn that she also speaks Japanese and Arabic.  Just as we begin to question how this young girl could be so talented as a spy-in-training we meet her deficiency.  She has had no human or world interaction. This plot element goes largely unexplained.  So if you craft questions about it while watching the movie, they will go unanswered.<br /><br /> The movie has notably two different tones.  One is reserved for the scenes in which we see Hanna interacting with the sights, sounds, and denizens of a world she only understood through her father's explanations as if they were fairytales. In fact, I hear it very easily. Varg's bass playing stands out in tracks like Pagan Fears, Freezing Moon and Buried By Time and Dust.<br /><br />The drums are played by least infamous member of the band, Hellhammer. He would reach newer heights with his ability in the future. Here he is more like a rabid machine more so than an actual human being. His drums here are not very diverse but they are very necessary and quite frankly the songs would only be half of what they are without them. He frantically pounds the living crap out of his drumset in every song. He lets up a tad on Freezing Moon and it is there where you hear some his best drum fills. His drums are very good, but he'd get even better.<br /><br />And last, the infamous vocals by the one and only Attila Csihar and for a lot of people; he is the breaking point of this album. He is what makes most people like or hate this record. For me, he made this record just as much as Euronymous, Varg and Hellhammer did. They all played their equal parts. Attila has some of the most ghoulish and frightening Black Metal vocals I've heard. He really just makes the songs his own *Although I love Dead too, but I don't compare them. It's like comparing Milk to Whiskey* and he has some very Dracula-esqe sounding clean vocals on the title track.<br /><br />There is not one bad song on here to me, I can listen to any of them and enjoy them as much as I did when I first heard this classic. This, along with Live In Leipzig, are what introduced me to Black Metal and I will forever hold them near the top of my \\"Classics\\" list.

Not NASCAR, mind you, but European style racing. If I could give you an exact name of the circuit, I would, but racing has never been my thing.    The story is really about Denny and the tragedies that befall his family, but thanks to the unique perspective there's always a funny and loving view of the events. The best thing a novel can do is elicit an emotional response from the reader, and The Art of Racing in the Rain does this incredibly well. It's a great book that will probably be appreciated more by dog-lovers or anyone who's owned a pet, yet I'd still recommend it to anyone else.

B004EQAVHI|5.0|*****Spoiler Alert***** Reveals plot.       I just found this gem, although it came out last year.       I don't intend to give a full scene by scene critique of the entire movie.  But I do need to speak of enough of it to give you an inkling of how and why I came to feel about this movie the way I do and maybe shine a sense on why it affected me the way it did, does, and why I can't stop looking at it over and over regardless of the way I know it is going to make me feel.  I will warn you that you watch this movie at your own risk.  It is rated R, but except for a few magazine pictures and very brief scenes of sexual content that some might find offensive, there is really nothing that should offend the senses in that respect.  I feel the rating is more for the emotions that it causes.  I certainly haven't anything affect me like this has but once before in my life.       The beginning of the movie made some general statements as to breakthroughs in medicine enabling the general lifespan to surpass 100 years.  We then go to an operating room where a woman begins talking about her being a carer and that she takes care of donors.  This then begins to make you wonder where this movie is going as you settle into watching a very British movie about children at a private school.  As the movie progressed, at one point it became apparent that the children were different in that they did not seem to have social skills beyond interacting with each other and the staff at the school.        Suffice it to say that at one point a new teacher arrives, who takes it upon herself to advise a group of 11 or 12 year olds that what they have been told but not told, if that makes any sense, is that their sole purpose in life will be to grow to adulthood and before reaching anything near to middle age begin to make organ donations so that others may live longer.  It then becomes apparent that these children have been cloned for the sole purpose of being donors.  That they will continue tomake periodic donations until their bodies can no longer sustain life, at which time they will be \\"completed.\\"  This teacher is soon fired.       The majority of the remainder of the movie shows these students (in particular, 2 girls and 1 boy), growing to adulthood, being sent to live in groups outside the many schools at which they were raised, upon turning 18, but still lacking in many social skills.  They do occasionally come into contact with others outside their groups, sometimes quite awkwardly.  This is not to say that they are not bright and articulate or do not have the same curiosities or urges as \\"normal people,\\" they have just not been taught to interact outside their groups socially, or to learn a profession, since their only purpose is to donate.  There are some who apply and become \\"carers\\" (helping others through the donations), but they all eventually become donors themselves.  Some form attachments for each other, fall in love, and seek ways to prolong their being able to stay together by obtaining a deferral (there are rumors of this being possible, which turn out to be false), from donation for a few years in order to be together.       In the end, all 3 have been separate from each other for 8 or 9 years or so.  One girl has become a carer and by accident meets the other girl, who has already made two donations.  They are then able to reunite with the boy, who also has made two donations.  Although the one girl who has begun donating and the boy at one time were together, it appears that it was the girl who is the carer and the boy who have always belonged together.  The girl admits that she had intentionally kept them apart because she was afraid of being alone.  The carer and the boy finally get together and decide to apply for a deferral.  She tells the other girl, who says she is happy for them.  The next scene (one that I have watched over and over), is that of the girl in an operating room making a donation, her third, and completing.  It is at this point that I really began to have problems with this movie.  Not with the movie itself, or the acting, or the way it looks.  I am very disturbed by the fact that a society would clone and raise living viable human beings to use as parts to prolong the lives of others.       It would be one thing to somehow produce organs, but living breathing thinking humans.  I know that this is only a movie, but to watch the way it is portrayed, to watch these young people calmly accept their fates and time after time going into an operation and donate knowing full well that at some point they will die, just makes no sense to me and saddens me to tears and gives me a feeling that I have difficulty breathing through.  To watch and listen as the one girls heart monitor escalates to a steady tone, to watch as they remove and bag some organ, to watch as they then turn off all of the machines and one by one everyone exits the operating room, leaving this beautiful young person lying alone on a gurney, thinking she has no family and wonder who will mourn her except possibly those who grew up with her and are soon to meet the same fate.  Truly, I can hardly see what I am writing, and my hands are shaking, too think that something like this would even be possible.  I do not think that I would care to live in a world with such values.       The carer and boy try to apply for a deferral and discover that none exist.  Here are the next two scenes that I watch over and over.  The boy finally goes in for a last donation, while she watches the operation through a window.  This is a return to the first scene of the movie.  The movie ends with her standing alone; looking over a landscape she refers to as her spot where she imagines where everything she has lost since her childhood has washed up.  She has received her notice of her first donation to occur soon.  She begins to think how it could have been, fantasizing about the boy coming back to her across the fields, and the scene fads out.       I went back and watched the scenes again, thinking everything would be okay.  Big mistake.  I don't think I have ever felt worse.       Watch at your own risk.  If you have watched this and weren't affected, there is more wrong with you than me.
