B003Y5H51K|3.0|Set in Papua New Guinea, Sanctum (2011) is a film about the struggle to survive in an elaborate system of underground tunnels.  It begins with a wide scope, focusing on a group of scientists exploring a huge cave, and then narrows down to center on a small group of survivors who become trapped underground.<br /><br />With James Cameron as executive producer, Sanctum has some nicely done underwater sequences, and some cinematic views of nature.  Unlike recent films like The Descent (2005) or The Cave (2005), Sanctum does not feature a threat from some kind of vicious creature.  Instead the danger comes mostly from natural causes.  While Sanctum scores some points in the technical departments, it doesn't exactly break a lot of new ground, and is also weak in character development, dialog, and realism.  The film tries hard, but doesn't quite succeed in telling a story that connects emotionally with the audience.<br /><br />Expert cave diver Frank McGuire (Richard Roxburgh) is the leader of an expedition exploring a huge subterranean cave, which consists of a system of tunnels, with several sections that are flooded with water.  With bad weather on the way, the team is hurrying to complete their work, before the rains come.  McGuire's son Josh (Rhys Wakefield), has just arrived back at camp with Carl Hurley (Ioan Gruffudd), the man bankrolling the expedition, and Victoria Elaine (Alice Parkinson) his girlfriend.<br /><br />Incredibly, those at ground level loose communication with the team below, and are unable to warn them that a huge storm has suddenly arrived, and heavy rains soon begin to flood the cave.  Josh doesn't have the best relationship with his father, and they have an argument after a diver accidentally dies.  Josh is on his way out of the cave, but returns to make an unsuccessful attempt to get his father and others on the lower level out, before they are trapped.  Instead, Josh finds himself among those imprisoned, with the route to the surface blocked by water.<br /><br />Frank immediately takes control of the situation, euthanizing a friend who is gravely injured.  Although he has no solid information about the weather conditions, McGuire feels that the group must continue to descend to avoid being drowned, and somehow find a path to the sea.  The survivors include Josh, Carl, Victoria, and Crazy George (Dan Wyllie), a long time member of McGuire's crew who has been banned from diving for health reasons.  Although she has no diving experience, Victoria manages to make it through a tight squeeze in an underwater cave, as the group emerges in an unexplored underground cavern.  As Frank continues to push forward, constantly barking orders like a drill sergeant, conditions soon begin to worsen.<br /><br />Once the explorers are trapped, no attention whatsoever is given to anyone outside of the cave, until the very end of the film.  While the survivors may be on their own, and a rescue attempt might be futile, totally ignoring those above seems odd.  There is a brief interlude when the survivors can see the sky, as they explore the remains of a tank from World War II, but they choose to reenter the caves and continue on an underground path.  You have to cut filmmakers some slack for any film set in a cave, as the lighting is almost never realistic.  Even so, Sanctum lacks realism in many other ways.  Swimming in chilly water, the survivors should all be freezing, but only Victoria is shivering.  These people are constantly wet, yet again, only Victoria shows any signs of being cold.  There's a lot of skin exposed, but the group remains injury and scrape free, except for a small mark of Josh's arm.  In general, the geography is pretty whacked, as the group conveniently finds an unlikely path to the sea.<br /><br />Besides a tale of survival, Sanctum is also about the evolution of Frank and Josh's relationship in a crisis.  The situation is rather clich&eacute;d, as Frank's passion for exploring caves has cost him his family.  The explorer comes across as such an arrogant dislikeable bastard, as Josh and the rest, discover just how hard survival can be.  Enduring the physical hardships involved in making the film, the acting performances by the mostly Australian cast are generally good, probably doing justice to the implausible script.  Rhys Wakefield is a young actor with a lot of potential and good screen presence.  Alice Parkinson is a stunning beauty, who is put through some tough physical trials, and hopefully will be seen much more in the future.  Ioan Gruffudd, known for playing Reed Richards, isn't quite as noble here.<br /><br />Coming into the project, director Alister Grierson did not have a lot of feature film experience, but he meets the challenge fairly well, delivering an entertaining, but flawed action/adventure that feels like a slightly manipulated ride.  With a little more thought and effort, Sanctum clearly could have achieved much more, but various plot issues keep the film from having more impact.uch an arrogant dislikeable bastard, as Josh and the rest, discover just how hard survival can be. I loved this new version because it poked fun at everything we take for granted today, everything that is silly about our society, and even gets in a few digs at the original movie with some well-placed tongue-in-cheek lines from the original.     The story line is different from the original but I liked the changes a lot. Colin Farrell makes a good, and pretty sexy, vamp! He plays the role of Jerry well and is very creepy. Still makes the same change from coward to courageous as in the old version. Sigh. And tight leather pants do help! Mmm. The connection could have been made earlier. Also, no explanation as to why Jerry didn't kill Peter when Peter was younger. Weird. The friends' back-story is fuller than in the original movie though and you really get a feeling that Ed wants revenge on Charlie for slighting him.    Special effects: Ok. When the heck are movie-writers going to stop using this daft \\"go-upstairs-to-get-away-from-the-bad-guy\\" routine. You would think the escapee would want to remain on the ground floor! Gah! His inhumanity rally gives him the jollies and makes his character much more slick and vampire-like. He acts like a true predator. The Jerry in the original film was more cautious and had a Renfield-like buddy to help him out during the day. I'm not sure why this helper character was done away with in this film but I think it was a pretty good choice. He always seemed extraneous in the original. B004BLJQOK|3.0|Seeing Let the Right One In two weeks before Let Me In makes for an interesting comparison. The original film, Let the Right One In, was an adaptation of a book that had complicated, uncomfortable themes that made it a challenge to adapt to the big screen.  This gave the Swedish film some rough edges: a prelude to a retrospective montage that never happened, Eli's androgyny, and the potentially unpleasant relationship between Eli and her \\"ghoul\\" (to use a Vampire RPG term).<br /><br />The androgynous nature of Eli changes her interactions with Oskar.  The mysterious relationship between Eli and her guardian let the viewer come to his or her own conclusions. Although Let the Right One In wasn't perfect, its flaws made it a better film. Let Me In smoothes over all these rough edges, for better or worse.<br /><br />The beautiful and deadly Chloe Grace Moretz (excellent as Hit Girl in Kick-Ass and equally breathtaking here) is Abby, an unearthly little girl who doesn't seem bothered by the cold, loves puzzles, and whose appearance veers from pretty to bedraggled. She befriends outcast Owen (Kodi Smit-McPhee, just as vulnerable here as he was in The Road), who dreams of stabbing the bullies who harass him.<br /><br />The subtle but important difference between the two films pivots on what the bullies call Owen (Oskar in the original): \\"little pig.\\" In the American version, Owen is called a little girl.  The gender appellation shifts the movie's focus from two lonely souls finding each other across the gulf of death to two Romeo and Juliet-style prepubescent tweens discovering romance. This doesn't lessen the emotional impact of Let the Right One, but it does change the film's tone significantly.<br /><br />With the exception of the cat sequence, which is missing entirely from Let Me In, Let the Right One In has creepier special effects.  Whether it's Eli/Abby's eyes flashing like a cat, the wreck of Hakan/Thomas' (Richard Jenkins) acid-ravaged face, or Virginia's (Sasha Barrese) horrible conflagration, Let Me In's digital effects look subpar in comparison. For some reason, all of Abby's attacks are handled digitally, which makes them surreal.<br /><br />Even the ending, which is almost a frame-for-frame reproduction, lacks the gut punch of the original by ratcheting back the gore. Let Me In fixes many discrepancies in Let the Right One In, but in doing so seems to be embarrassed by its Swedish roots.  But if you've never seen the original, Let Me In is an excellent substitute.n, Let Me In's digital effects look subpar in comparison. I wish the song and the verses were longer, but it's my favorite song on here.    Looking down the cross starts off slower, but builds to be a faster song later. It erupts into a really fast paced thrasher.  Very fun.  Killing is my business starts off kind of upper mid tempo then gets much faster.  Metallica never played as fast as this song or These boots.  Last rites/loved to death starts with a weird piano intro that blends into some guitar licks before the heaviness comes in.  the piano was kind of a welcome change, just to vary the sound up a bit.  Then it becomes a really fast song.  This one doesn't really do much for me.  Ah then there's Mechanix.  Dave decided since he wrote Four Horsemen, for Metallica that since he wrote it he was still going to use the song.  And, boy did he.  This one's much faster than the Metallica counterpart.  I actually only recognize it because of the chorus vocals.  Otherwise it sounds pretty different.  The lyrics are different as well, what with it technically suppose to be a different song.  Rattlehead is another really fast song.  This one also doesn't really grab me, but oh well.  It's there none the less.  Overall if you like Megadeth this is a definite must own.  If you're new to the band I'd recommend Rust in peace, or Countdown to extinction.  But don't pass this one up.

0393338827|4.0|On Thursday September 18, 2008 the market in derivatives tanked. It had been soft for several days. But on that Thursday it fell over the cliff; and before the dust settled several Wall Street landmarks were either no more or greatly changed.  Bear Sterns, Lehman Brothers, and Merrril Lynch were no more, AIG was underwater and the chances were good that the remaining bit firms still standing (J.P. Morgan and Goldman Sachs) had liquidity problems.  Furthermore the banks were in trouble: Countrywide, Wachovia and Washington Mutual were on their way out and a lot of others might have been as well had not the Government poured billions (the notorious TARP funds) into the financial system.      What might have happened had the Treasure not stepped in at that moment will never be known, but what is known is that today nearly three years later we are still living with the result- a continuing recession equaled in depth only by the Great Depression of the 1930s, constant and acerbic political division, a Federal Government which appears so dysfunctional that one wonders whether it can continue to exist for many more years without substantial Constitutional change - And there is no end in sight     One of the contributing causes of this - if not the immediate precipitating cause of that September day - was spendthrift, uncontrolled lending in the housing market which had inflated home prices well beyond the limits of financial sanity Even with virtually no assets and maybe even no job you could get a 6% thirty year first mortgage with nothing down and no interest payments for two years. Then when the value of your home went up and you had additional equity equity you could re-finance, cash in the equity with another and bigger mortgage which stood on even shakier financial feet  Then you could use the cash you took out of the refinancing to put down on that nice new Ford pickup you always wanted or maybe spend a week or two in Hawaii. And the lender could sell your even-now- less-sub-prime mortgage to a financial house which was buying virtually sight unseen a lot of them not unlike yours The financial house would bundle them into a package, call it a bond and sell the bond as a sub prime mortgage bond - not, however, without first vetting the bond through Moody's or one of Moody's cousins with maybe an AAA rating. Not only was the bond vetted but also it could be insured through AIG.      Furthermore if you were a buyer and savvy enough you didn't even have to buy the sub prime bond. You could buy a credit default swap which cost you maybe one percent per year of the face amount of the bond you didn't have or were short on and promised to pay off the face value (less residual value) if and when it defaulted or was redeemed     These instruments  - the sub-prime mortgage bond  and the credit default swap - were derivatives.  i.e. they derived their value from the bundle of mortgages which \\"secured\\" each bond.      However all of the mortgages in the original bundle were not of the same value, the same safety. The bundle could be separated into sub-bundles and a sub bundle could be the subject of a separate derivative. -  a CDO or collateralized debt obligation which represented the triple B rated bundle of mortgages at the bottom of the original bundle. However through the CDO which would obtain a triple A rating on its own, the less valuable mortgages would have a new though fraudulent life as respectable security      To some very smart young people who are the subjects of Michael Lewis's The Big Short, this was like finding money in the street. You had a triple a bond secured by what you knew was trash and it was insured; or you might have credit default swaps on such bond. You knew the bond was never going the full 30 years.  So you bought credit default swaps and went short on the bonds and waited for the crash. When it came in September 2008 you made a mint. This was The Big Short.      Michael Lewis is one of those very few financial writers who has the unique ability to write clearly and with humor about he most complicated of financial mattress and he is at his best in this book. It's written with zest and style  - and he tells it like it is -  or was.      Looking back it's easy to follow his trail of words and see with terrible clarity the inexcusable stupidity of most or our leading financial houses, but then the same is true in any bubble - whether you look back on the tech bubble of 2000, utility stocks in 1929, railroad stocks in 1890 or tulips in Holland two centuries ago.       The whole thing might be funny if the results had not been so tragic. People are out of work and out of their homes. The Federal deficit is out of control because, among other things, of the money it poured into the economy to prop up the remaining banks - and which at the moment doesn't seem to have done a lot of good. You can get a sense of why this all exists by reading The Big Short.
